      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macroeconomics">Macroeconomics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Macroeconomics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>According to the government of Transnistria, the 2007 GDP was 6789 mln Transnistrian roubles (appx US$799&nbsp;million) and the GDP per capita was about US$1,500. The GDP increased by 11.1% and inflation rate was 19.3% with the GDP per capita now being $2,140, higher than Moldova's GDP per capita that is $2,040.<sup id="cite_ref-stat2007_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stat2007-113">[112]</a></sup> Transnistria's government budget for 2007 was US$246&nbsp;million, with an estimated deficit of about US$100&nbsp;million<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> that the government planned to cover with income from privatisations.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> The budget for 2008 is US$331&nbsp;million, with an estimated deficit of about US$80&nbsp;million.<sup id="cite_ref-nr2_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nr2-116">[115]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2004, Transnistria had debts of US$1.2&nbsp;billion (two-thirds are with Russia) that was per capita about six times higher than in Moldova (without Transnistria).<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> In March 2007 the debt to <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a> for the acquisition of natural gas increased to US$1.3&nbsp;billion. On 22 March 2007 Gazprom sold Transnistria's gas debt to the Russian businessman <a href="/wiki/Alisher_Usmanov" title="Alisher Usmanov">Alisher Usmanov</a>, who controls <a href="/wiki/Moldova_Steel_Works" title="Moldova Steel Works">Moldova Steel Works</a>, the largest enterprise in Transnistria. Transnistria's president Igor Smirnov has announced that Transnistria will not pay its gas debt because "Transnistria has no legal debt to Gazprom".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> In November 2007, the total debt of Transnistria's public sector was up to US$1.64&nbsp;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-nr2_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nr2-116">[115]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to a 2007 interview with <a href="/wiki/Yevgeny_Shevchuk" title="Yevgeny Shevchuk">Yevgeny Shevchuk</a>, the then-speaker of the <a href="/wiki/Transnistrian_Supreme_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Transnistrian Supreme Council">Transnistrian Supreme Council</a>, Transnistria is in a difficult economic situation. Despite a 30% tax increase in 2007, the pension fund is still lacking money and emergency measures must be taken.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> However, Shevchuk mentioned that the situation is not hopeless and it cannot be considered a crisis, as a crisis means three-month delays in payment of pensions and salaries.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="External_trade">External trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: External trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/Transnistrian_Republican_Bank" title="Transnistrian Republican Bank">Transnistrian Republican Bank</a> reported exports of US$422.0&nbsp;million and imports of US$738.4&nbsp;million. Compared to a year prior, export decreased 27.2% and import decreased 13.7%. The trade deficit reached US$316.3&nbsp;million.<sup id="cite_ref-bank95_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bank95-122">[121]</a></sup> Over 50% of the export went to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">CIS</a>, mainly to Russia, but also to Belarus, Ukraine, and Moldova (which Transnistrian authorities consider foreign).<sup id="cite_ref-icg_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-icg-103">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-viitorul_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-viitorul-104">[103]</a></sup> Main non-CIS markets for the Transnistrian goods were Italy, Egypt, Greece, Romania, and Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-icg_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-icg-103">[102]</a></sup> The CIS accounted for over 60% of the imports, while the share of the EU was about 23%. The main imports were non-precious metals, food products, and electricity. </p>
      <p>After Moldova signed the Association Agreement with the EU in 2014, Transnistria—being <i>de jure</i> part of Moldova—enjoyed the tariff-free exports to the EU. As a result, in 2015, 27% of Transnistria's US$189 million exports went to the EU, while exports to Russia went down to 7.7%. This shift towards the EU market continued to grow in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_sectors">Economic sectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Economic sectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The leading industry is steel, due to the <a href="/wiki/Moldova_Steel_Works" title="Moldova Steel Works">Moldova Steel Works</a> (part of the Russian <a href="/wiki/Metalloinvest" title="Metalloinvest">Metalloinvest</a> holding) in <a href="/wiki/R%C3%AEbni%C8%9Ba" title="Rîbnița">Rîbnița</a>, which accounts for about 60% of the budget revenue of Transnistria.<sup id="cite_ref-msca_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msca-75">[74]</a></sup> The largest company in the textile industry is <a href="/wiki/Tirotex" title="Tirotex">Tirotex</a>, which claims to be the second largest textile company in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> The energy sector is dominated by Russian companies. The largest power company Moldavskaya GRES (<a href="/wiki/Kuchurgan_power_station" title="Kuchurgan power station">Kuchurgan power station</a>) is in <a href="/wiki/Dnestrovsc" title="Dnestrovsc">Dnestrovsc</a> and owned by <a href="/wiki/Inter_RAO" title="Inter RAO">Inter RAO</a> UES,<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> and the gas transmission and distribution company <a href="/wiki/Tiraspoltransgas" title="Tiraspoltransgas">Tiraspoltransgas</a> is probably controlled by <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a>, although Gazprom has not confirmed the ownership officially. The banking sector of Transnistria consists of 8 commercial banks, including <a href="/wiki/Gazprombank_(Transnistria)" title="Gazprombank (Transnistria)">Gazprombank</a>. The oldest alcohol producer <a href="/wiki/KVINT" title="KVINT">KVINT</a>, located in Tiraspol, produces and exports brandy, wines and vodka. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Transnistria" title="Human rights in Transnistria">Human rights in Transnistria</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The human rights record of Transnistria has been criticised by several governments and international organisations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (March 2013)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> The 2007 <i><a href="/wiki/Freedom_in_the_World" title="Freedom in the World">Freedom in the World</a></i> report, published by the U.S.-based <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>, described Transnistria as a "non-free" territory, having an equally bad situation in both political rights and civil liberties.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of State">U.S. Department of State</a> report referring to the year 2006:<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>The right of citizens to change their government was restricted.... Authorities reportedly continued to use torture and <a href="/wiki/Arbitrary_arrest_and_detention" title="Arbitrary arrest and detention">arbitrary arrest and detention</a>.... In Transnistria authorities limited freedom of speech and of the press....Authorities usually did not permit free assembly.... In the separatist region of Transnistria the authorities continued to deny registration and harassed a number of minority religious groups.... The separatist region remained a significant source and transit area for trafficking in persons.... Homosexuality was illegal, and gays and lesbians were subject to governmental and societal discrimination.</p>
      </blockquote> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_in_Transnistria" class="mw-redirect" title="Media in Transnistria">Media in Transnistria</a>
      </div> 
      <p>There is a regular mix of modern news media in Transnistria with a number of television stations, newspapers, and radio stations. </p>
      <p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe</a> (OSCE) the media climate in Transnistria is restrictive and the authorities continue a long-standing campaign to silence independent opposition voices and groups.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to a US Department of State report for 2006, "Both of the region's major newspapers were controlled by the authorities. There was one independent weekly newspaper in Bender and another in the northern city of Rîbnița.... Separatist authorities harassed independent newspapers for critical reporting of the Transnistrian regime.... Most television and radio stations and print publication were controlled by Transnistrian authorities, which largely dictated their editorial policies and finance operations. Some broadcast networks, such as the TSV television station and the INTER-FM radio station, were owned by Transnistria's largest monopoly, <a href="/wiki/Sheriff_(company)" title="Sheriff (company)">Sheriff</a>, which also holds a majority in the region's legislature.... In July 2005 the Transnistrian Supreme Council amended the election code to prohibit media controlled by the Transnistrian authorities from publishing results of polls and forecasts related to elections."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-129">[128]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanian-language_schools">Romanian-language schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Romanian-language schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romanian-language_schools_in_Transnistria" title="Romanian-language schools in Transnistria">Romanian-language schools in Transnistria</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-Romanian_discrimination" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Romanian discrimination">Anti-Romanian discrimination</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Public education in the <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian language</a> (officially called <a href="/wiki/Moldovan_language" title="Moldovan language">Moldovan language</a> in Transnistria) is done using the Soviet-originated <a href="/wiki/Moldovan_Cyrillic_alphabet" title="Moldovan Cyrillic alphabet">Moldovan Cyrillic alphabet</a>. The usage of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> was restricted to only six schools. <a href="/wiki/Romanian-language_schools_in_Transnistria" title="Romanian-language schools in Transnistria">Four of these schools</a> were forcibly closed by the authorities, who claimed this was due to the refusal of the schools to apply for official accreditation.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> These schools were later registered as private schools and reopened, which may have been accelerated by pressure from the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The OSCE mission to Moldova has urged local authorities in the Transnistrian city of Rîbnița to return a confiscated building to the Moldovan Latin script school in the city. The unfinished building was nearing completion in 2004, when Transnistria took control of it during that year's school crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In November 2005 <a href="/wiki/Ion_Iovcev" title="Ion Iovcev">Ion Iovcev</a>, the principal of a Romanian-language school in Transnistria and active advocate for human rights as well as a critic of the Transnistrian leadership, received threatening calls that he attributed to his criticism of the separatist regime.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-129">[128]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arms_control_and_disarmament">Arms control and disarmament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Arms control and disarmament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Transnistria" title="Crime in Transnistria">Crime in Transnistria</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Following the collapse of the former Soviet Union, the Russian 14th Army left 40,000 tonnes of weaponry and ammunition in Transnistria. In later years there were concerns<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> that the Transnistrian authorities would try to sell these stocks internationally, and intense pressure was applied to have these removed by <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. </p>
      <p>In 2000 and 2001, Russia withdrew by rail 141 self-propelled artillery pieces and other armoured vehicles and destroyed locally, 108 <a href="/wiki/T-64_tank" class="mw-redirect" title="T-64 tank">T-64 tanks</a> and 139 other pieces of military equipment limited by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_Conventional_Armed_Forces_in_Europe" title="Treaty on Conventional Armed Forces in Europe">Treaty on Conventional Armed Forces in Europe</a> (CFE). During 2002 and 2003 Russian military officials destroyed a further 51 armoured vehicles, all of which were types not limited by the CFE Treaty. The OSCE also observed and verified the withdrawal of 48 trains with military equipment and ammunition in 2003. However, no further withdrawal activities have taken place since March 2004 and a further 20,000 tons of ammunition, as well as some remaining military equipment, are still to be removed. </p>
      <p>In the autumn of 2006, the Transnistrian leadership agreed to let an OSCE inspectorate examine the munitions and further access was agreed moving forward. </p>
      <p>Recent weapons inspections were permitted by Transnistria and conducted by the OSCE. The onus of responsibility rests on Russia to remove the rest of the supplies. </p>
      <p>Transnistrian authorities declared that they are not involved in the manufacture or export of weapons. OSCE and European Union officials stated in 2005 that there is no evidence that Transnistria "has ever trafficked arms or nuclear material" and much of the alarm is due to the Moldovan government's attempts to pressure Transnistria.<sup id="cite_ref-feg_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feg-133">[132]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2007, foreign experts working on behalf of the United Nations said that the historically low levels of transparency and continued denial of full investigations to international monitors have reinforced negative perceptions of the Transnistrian government, although recent co-operation by Transnistrian authorities may have reflected a shift in the attitude of Transnistria.<sup id="cite_ref-seesac_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seesac-134">[133]</a></sup> Their report stated that the evidence for the illicit production and <a href="/wiki/Trafficking_of_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Trafficking of weapons">trafficking of weapons</a> into and from Transnistria, has in the past been exaggerated, although the trafficking of light weapons is likely to have occurred before 2001 (the last year when export data showed US$900,000 worth of 'weapons, munitions, their parts and accessories' exported from Transnistria). The report also states that the same holds true for the production of such weapons, which is likely to have been carried out in the 1990s, primarily to equip Transnistrian forces. </p>
      <p>The OSCE mission spokesman Claus Neukirch spoke about this situation: "There is often talk about sale of armaments from Transnistria, but there is no convincing evidence."<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2010, Viktor Kryzhanovskyi, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>'s special envoy on Transnistria, stated that there was no ongoing <a href="/wiki/Arms_trafficking" title="Arms trafficking">arms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Drug_trafficking" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug trafficking">drug trafficking</a> through the Transnistrian section of the Ukrainian-Moldovan border at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Спецпредставитель_Украины_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Спецпредставитель_Украины-136">[135]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r936637989">.mw-parser-output .portal{border:solid #aaa 1px;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li>span:first-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li>span:last-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}</style>
      <div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright"> 
       <ul> 
        <li><span><img alt="map" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Europe_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/28px-Europe_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="28" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Europe_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/42px-Europe_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Europe_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/56px-Europe_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="541" data-file-height="541"></span><span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Europe" title="Portal:Europe">Europe portal</a></span></li>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transnistria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

       <li>Beyer, John, and Stefan Wolff. "Linkage and leverage effects on Moldova's Transnistria problem." <i>East European Politics</i> 32.3 (2016): 335–354 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/pdf/10.1080/21599165.2015.1124092">online</a>.</li> 
       <li>Blakkisrud, Helge, and Pål Kolstø. "From secessionist conflict toward a functioning state: processes of state-and nation-building in Transnistria." <i>Post-Soviet Affairs</i> 27.2 (2011): 178–210 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.academia.edu/download/59478168/Post-Soviet_Affairs_Blakkisrud___Kolsto_PMR_from_secessionist20190601-116312-ykxnkt.pdf">online</a>.</li> 
       <li>Cojocaru, Natalia. "Nationalism and identity in Transnistria." <i>Innovation</i> 19.3–4 (2006): 261–272 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.offiziere.ch/wp-content/uploads/13511610601029813.pdf">online</a>.</li> 
       <li>Lynch, Dov. <i>Russian peacekeeping strategies in the CIS: the case of Moldova, Georgia and Tajikistan</i> (Springer, 1999).</li> 
       <li>Protsyk, Oleh. "Representation and democracy in Eurasia's unrecognized states: The case of Transnistria." <i>Post-Soviet Affairs</i> 25.3 (2009): 257–281 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.policy.hu/protsyk/Publications/Articles/PSATrans09.pdf">online</a>.</li> 
       <li>RAND, <i>Russia’s Hostile Measures: Combating Russian Gray Zone Aggression Against NATO in the Contact, Blunt, and Surge Layers of Competition</i> (2020) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.rand.org/t/RR2539">online</a> on Transnistria</li>

          <th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="width:1%"><a href="/wiki/History_of_Transnistria" title="History of Transnistria">History</a></th>
          <td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
           <div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
